<script>
    (function () {
        if ( typeof window.CustomEvent === "function" ) return false;
        function CustomEvent ( event, params ) {
            params = params || { bubbles: false, cancelable: false, detail: null };
            var evt = document.createEvent( 'CustomEvent' );
            evt.initCustomEvent( event, params.bubbles, params.cancelable, params.detail );
            return evt;
        }

        CustomEvent.prototype = window.Event.prototype;

        window.CustomEvent = CustomEvent;
    })();
</script>

    <meta name="journal_id" content="41588"/>

    <meta name="dc.title" content="The draft genome of a diploid cotton Gossypium raimondii"/>

    <meta name="dc.source" content="Nature Genetics 2012 44:10"/>

    <meta name="dc.format" content="text/html"/>

    <meta name="dc.publisher" content="Nature Publishing Group"/>

    <meta name="dc.date" content="2012-08-26"/>

    <meta name="dc.type" content="OriginalPaper"/>

    <meta name="dc.language" content="En"/>

    <meta name="dc.copyright" content="2012 The Author(s)"/>

    <meta name="dc.rightsAgent" content="journalpermissions@springernature.com"/>

    <meta name="dc.description" content="Yuxian Zhu and colleagues report the draft genome of a diploid cotton Gossypium raimondii. This species is a wild South American cotton, whose progenitor is thought to have been the contributor of the D subgenome of the allotetraploid commercial species Gossypium hirsutum and Gossypium barbadense, which account for ~95% of the worldwide cotton crop. We have sequenced and assembled a draft genome of G. raimondii, whose progenitor is the putative contributor of the D subgenome to the economically important fiber-producing cotton species Gossypium hirsutum and Gossypium barbadense. Over 73% of the assembled sequences were anchored on 13 G. raimondii chromosomes. The genome contains 40,976 protein-coding genes, with 92.2% of these further confirmed by transcriptome data. Evidence of the hexaploidization event shared by the eudicots as well as of a cotton-specific whole-genome duplication approximately 13&#8211;20 million years ago was observed. We identified 2,355 syntenic blocks in the G. raimondii genome, and we found that approximately 40% of the paralogous genes were present in more than 1 block, which suggests that this genome has undergone substantial chromosome rearrangement during its evolution. Cotton, and probably Theobroma cacao, are the only sequenced plant species that possess an authentic CDN1 gene family for gossypol biosynthesis, as revealed by phylogenetic analysis."/>

This species is a wild South American cotton, whose progenitor is thought to have been the contributor of the D subgenome of the allotetraploid commercial species Gossypium hirsutum and Gossypium barbadense, which account for ~95% of the worldwide cotton crop."/>

            <div class="c-article-body">
                <section aria-labelledby="Abs2" data-title="Abstract" lang="en"><div class="c-article-section" id="Abs2-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Abs2">Abstract</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Abs2-content"><p>We have sequenced and assembled a draft genome of <i>G. raimondii</i>, whose progenitor is the putative contributor of the D subgenome to the economically important fiber-producing cotton species <i>Gossypium hirsutum</i> and <i>Gossypium barbadense</i>. Over 73% of the assembled sequences were anchored on 13 <i>G. raimondii</i> chromosomes. The genome contains 40,976 protein-coding genes, with 92.2% of these further confirmed by transcriptome data. Evidence of the hexaploidization event shared by the eudicots as well as of a cotton-specific whole-genome duplication approximately 13â20 million years ago was observed. We identified 2,355 syntenic blocks in the <i>G. raimondii</i> genome, and we found that approximately 40% of the paralogous genes were present in more than 1 block, which suggests that this genome has undergone substantial chromosome rearrangement during its evolution. Cotton, and probably <i>Theobroma cacao</i>, are the only sequenced plant species that possess an authentic <i>CDN1</i> gene family for gossypol biosynthesis, as revealed by phylogenetic analysis.</p></div></div></section>
                <noscript>
                    
                </noscript>

                    <section aria-labelledby="Sec1" data-title="Main"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec1">Main</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec1-content"><p>Cotton is one of the most economically important crop plants worldwide. Its fiber, commonly known as cotton lint, is the principal natural source for the textile industry. Approximately 33 million ha (5% of the world's arable land) is used for cotton planting<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1" title="Jia, S. et al. Transgenic Cotton. Ch. 1, 8â17 (Science Press, Beijing and New York, 2006)." href="/articles/ng.2371#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d22850e727">1</a></sup>, with an annual global market value of textile mills of approximately $630.6 billion in 2011 (MarketPublishers; see URLs). Apart from its economic value, cotton is also an excellent model system for studying polyploidization, cell elongation and cell wall biosynthesis<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2" title="Ruan, Y.L., Llewellyn, D.J. &amp; Furbank, R.T. Suppression of sucrose synthase gene expression represses cotton fiber cell initiation, elongation, and seed development. Plant Cell 15, 952â964 (2003)." href="/articles/ng.2371#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d22850e731">2</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 3" title="Shi, Y.H. et al. Transcriptome profiling, molecular biological, and physiological studies reveal a major role for ethylene in cotton fiber cell elongation. Plant Cell 18, 651â664 (2006)." href="/articles/ng.2371#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d22850e734">3</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Wang, S. et al. Control of plant trichome development by a cotton fiber MYB gene. Plant Cell 16, 2323â2334 (2004)." href="/articles/ng.2371#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d22850e737">4</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="Qin, Y.M. &amp; Zhu, Y.X. How cotton fibers elongate: a tale of linear cell-growth mode. Curr. Opin. Plant Biol. 14, 106â111 (2011)." href="/articles/ng.2371#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d22850e740">5</a></sup>.</p><p>The <i>Gossypium</i> genus contains 5 tetraploid (AD<sub>1</sub> to AD<sub>5</sub>, 2<i>n</i> = 4Ã) and over 45 diploid (2<i>n</i> = 2Ã) species (where <i>n</i> is the number of chromosomes in the gamete of an individual), which are believed to have originated from a common ancestor approximately 5â10 million years ago<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Wendel, J.F. &amp; Albert, V.A. Phylogenetics of the cotton genus (Gossypium): character-state weighted parsimony analysis of chloroplast-DNA restriction site data and its systematic and biogeographic implications. Syst. Bot. 17, 115â143 (1992)." href="/articles/ng.2371#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d22850e764">6</a></sup>. Eight diploid subgenomes, designated as A to G and K, have been found across North America, Africa, Asia and Australia. The haploid genome size of diploid cottons (2<i>n</i> = 2Ã = 26) varies from about 880 Mb (<i>G. raimondii</i> Ulbrich) in the D genome to 2,500 Mb in the K genome<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 7" title="Hawkins, J.S. et al. Differential lineage-specific amplification of transposable elements is responsible for genome size variation in Gossypium. Genome Res. 16, 1252â1261 (2006)." href="/articles/ng.2371#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d22850e774">7</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Hendrix, B. &amp; Stewart, J.M. Estimation of the nuclear DNA content of Gossypium species. Ann. Bot. 95, 789â797 (2005)." href="/articles/ng.2371#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d22850e777">8</a></sup>. Diploid cotton species share a common chromosome number (<i>n</i> = 13), and high levels of synteny or colinearity are observed among them<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 9" title="Rong, J. et al. A 3347-locus genetic recombination map of sequence-tagged sites reveals features of genome organization, transmission and evolution of cotton (Gossypium). Genetics 166, 389â417 (2004)." href="/articles/ng.2371#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d22850e785">9</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Reinisch, A.J. et al. A detailed RFLP map of cotton, G. hirsutum Ã G. barbadense: chromosome organization and evolution in a disomic polyploidy genome. Genetics 138, 829â847 (1994)." href="/articles/ng.2371#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d22850e788">10</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="Brubaker, C.L., Paterson, A.H. &amp; Wendel, J.F. Comparative genetic mapping of allotetraploid cotton and its diploid progenitors. Genome 42, 184â203 (1999)." href="/articles/ng.2371#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d22850e791">11</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 12" title="Desai, A., Chee, P.W., Rong, J., May, O.L. &amp; Paterson, A.H. Chromosome structural changes in diploid and tetraploid A genomes of Gossypium. Genome 49, 336â345 (2006)." href="/articles/ng.2371#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d22850e794">12</a></sup>. The tetraploid cotton species (2<i>n</i> = 4Ã = 52), such as <i>G. hirsutum</i> L. and <i>Gossypium barbadense</i> L., are thought to have formed by an allopolyploidization event that occurred approximately 1â2 million years ago, which involved a D-genome species as the pollen-providing parent and an A-genome species as the maternal parent<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 13" title="Sunilkumar, G., Campbell, L.A.M., Puckhaber, L., Stipanovic, R.D. &amp; Rathore, K.S. Engineering cottonseed for use in human nutrition by tissue-specific reduction of toxic gossypol. Proc. Natl. Acad. Sci. USA 103, 18054â18059 (2006)." href="/articles/ng.2371#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d22850e807">13</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Chen, Z.J. et al. Toward sequencing cotton (Gossypium) genomes. Plant Physiol. 145, 1303â1310 (2007)." href="/articles/ng.2371#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d22850e810">14</a></sup>. To gain insights into the cultivated polyploid genomesâhow they have evolved and how their subgenomes interactâit is first necessary to have a basic knowledge of the structure of the component genomes. Therefore, we have created a draft sequence of the putative D-genome parent, <i>G. raimondii</i>, using DNA samples prepared from Cotton Microsatellite Database (CMD) 10 (refs. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 15" title="Yu, J.Z. A standard panel of Gossypium genotypes established for systematic characterization of cotton microsatellite markers. Plant Breeding News 148, 1.07 (2004)." href="/articles/ng.2371#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d22850e817">15</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 16" title="Blenda, A. et al. CMD: a cotton microsatellite database resource for Gossypium genomics. BMC Genomics 7, 132â141 (2006)." href="/articles/ng.2371#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d22850e820">16</a>), a genetic standard originated from a single seed (accession D<sub>5</sub>-3) in 2004 and brought to near homozygosity by six successive generations of self-fertilization. We believe that sequencing of the <i>G. raimondii</i> genome will not only provide a major source of candidate genes important for the genetic improvement of cotton quality and productivity, but it may also serve as a reference for the assembly of the tetraploid <i>G. hirsutum</i> genome.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec2" data-title="Results"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec2-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec2">Results</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec2-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec3">Sequencing and assembly</h3><p>A whole-genome shotgun strategy was used to sequence and assemble the <i>G. raimondii</i> genome. A total of 78.7 Gb of next-generation Illumina paired-end 50-bp, 100-bp and 150-bp reads was generated by sequencing genome shotgun libraries of different fragment lengths (170 bp, 250 bp, 500 bp, 800 bp, 2 kb, 5 kb, 10 kb, 20 kb and 40 kb) that covered 103.6-fold of the 775.2-Mb assembled <i>G. raimondii</i> genome (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2371#MOESM1">Supplementary Table 1</a>). The resulting assembly appeared to cover a very large proportion of the euchromatin of the <i>G. raimondii</i> genome. The unassembled genomic regions are likely to contain heterochromatic satellites, large repetitive sequences or ribosomal RNA (rRNA) genes. Using a set of 1,369 molecular markers from a consensus genetic linkage map reported previously<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="Lin, L. et al. A draft physical map of a D-genome cotton species (Gossypium raimondii). BMC Genomics 11, 395 (2010)." href="/articles/ng.2371#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d22850e856">17</a></sup>, 43.8% of the markers (599) were unambiguously located on the assembly, allowing us to anchor 73.2% of the assembled 567.2 Mb on the <i>G. raimondii</i> chromosomes (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2371#MOESM1">Supplementary Fig. 1</a>).</p><p>The assembly, performed by SOAPdenovo<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 18" title="Li, R. et al. The sequence and de novo assembly of the giant panda genome. Nature 463, 311â317 (2010)." href="/articles/ng.2371#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d22850e870">18</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 19" title="Li, R. et al. De novo assembly of human genomes with massively parallel short read sequencing. Genome Res. 20, 265â272 (2010)." href="/articles/ng.2371#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d22850e873">19</a></sup>, consisted of 41,307 contigs and 4,715 scaffolds and accounted for approximately 88.1% of the estimated <i>G. raimondii</i> genome<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Hendrix, B. &amp; Stewart, J.M. Estimation of the nuclear DNA content of Gossypium species. Ann. Bot. 95, 789â797 (2005)." href="/articles/ng.2371#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d22850e880">8</a></sup> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/ng.2371#Tab1">Table 1</a>). Over 73% of the assembly was in 281 chromosome-anchored scaffolds, with 228 of them both anchored and oriented (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2371#MOESM1">Supplementary Fig. 1</a>). The N50 (the size above which 50% of the total length of the sequence assembly can be found) of contigs and scaffolds was 44.9 kb and 2,284 kb, respectively, with the largest scaffold measuring 12.8 Mb (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2371#MOESM1">Supplementary Table 2</a>). As indicated by sequencing depth distribution analysis, 98.8% of the assembly was sequenced at 10Ã coverage (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2371#MOESM1">Supplementary Fig. 2</a>). Of the 58,061 ESTs (&gt;500 bp in length) reported in <i>G. raimondii</i>, 93.4% were identified in the assembly (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2371#MOESM1">Supplementary Table 3</a>). Sequences of 24 of the 25 randomly selected, completely sequenced <i>G. raimondii</i> BAC clones downloaded from GenBank (<a href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/nuccore/?term=AC243106">AC243106</a> â <a href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/nuccore/?term=AC243130">AC243130</a>) were fully recovered from our assembly (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2371#MOESM1">Supplementary Table 4</a>), supporting the view that the <i>G. raimondii</i> genome was assembled properly. Percentagewise, coding regions (exons), introns, DNA transposable elements, long terminal repeats (LTRs) and other repeat sequences made up 6.4%, 6.9%, 4.4%, 42.6% and 13.0% of the total genome content, respectively (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.2371#Fig1">Fig. 1</a>). On most <i>G. raimondii</i> chromosomes, genes were more abundant in the subtelomeric regions (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.2371#Fig1">Fig. 1</a>), as previously reported for <i>T. cacao</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 20" title="Argout, X. et al. The genome of Theobroma cacao. Nat. Genet. 43, 101â108 (2011)." href="/articles/ng.2371#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d22850e939">20</a></sup> and <i>Zea mays</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 21" title="Schnable, P.S. et al. The B73 maize genome: complexity, diversity, and dynamics. Science 326, 1112â1115 (2009)." href="/articles/ng.2371#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d22850e945">21</a></sup>. Transposable elements were distributed largely in gene-poor regions (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.2371#Fig1">Fig. 1</a>).</p><div class="c-article-table" data-test="inline-table" data-container-section="table" id="table-1"><figure><figcaption class="c-article-table__figcaption"><b id="Tab1" data-test="table-caption">Table 1 Global statistics for the <i>G. raimondii</i> genome assembly and annotation</b></figcaption><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="table-link" xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-1" data-title="Genomic overview of the 13 assembled G. raimondii chromosomes."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig1" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 1: Genomic overview of the 13 assembled <i>G. raimondii</i> chromosomes.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ng.2371/figures/1" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fng.2371/MediaObjects/41588_2012_Article_BFng2371_Fig1_HTML.jpg?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-1-desc" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-1-desc"><p>Major DNA components are categorized into exons, introns, DNA transposable elements (TEs), LTRs (retrotransposons) and other (repeat sequence other than DNA TEs and LTRs). Gray color indicates DNA elements not defined by the previous five terms. All categories were determined for 1.0-Mb windows with a 0.05-Mb shift.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ng.2371/figures/1" data-track-dest="link:Figure1 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec4">Gene content, annotation and analysis of major gene families</h3><p>Genome annotation was performed by combining results obtained from <i>ab initio</i> prediction, homology search and EST alignment. We identified 40,976 protein-coding genes in the <i>G. raimondii</i> genome, with an average transcript size of 2,485 bp (GLEAN) and a mean of 4.5 exons per gene (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/ng.2371#Tab1">Table 1</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2371#MOESM1">Supplementary Table 5</a>). There were 348 micorRNAs (miRNAs), 565 rRNAs, 1,041 tRNAs and 1,082 small nuclear RNAs (snRNAs) in the <i>G. raimondii</i> genome (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/ng.2371#Tab1">Table 1</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2371#MOESM1">Supplementary Table 6</a>). Among the annotated genes, 83.69% encode proteins that show homology to proteins in the TrEMBL database, and 69.98% were identified in InterPro (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2371#MOESM1">Supplementary Table 7</a>). As a result, 71.68% of the predicted genes were supported by at least two methods (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2371#MOESM1">Supplementary Table 8</a>). Overall, 92.2% (37,780 of 40,976) of predicted coding sequences from the genome were supported by transcriptome sequencing data (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2371#MOESM1">Supplementary Fig. 3</a>), which showed the high accuracy of <i>G. raimondii</i> gene predictions. Compared to the smaller <i>Arabidopsis thaliana</i> genome<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 22" title="The Arabidopsis Genome Initiative. Analysis of the genome sequence of the flowering plant Arabidopsis thaliana. Nature 408, 796â815 (2000)." href="/articles/ng.2371#ref-CR22" id="ref-link-section-d22850e1392">22</a></sup>, the <i>G. raimondii</i> genome had a higher gene number, a similar exon number per gene and a lower mean gene density per 100 kb of genomic DNA sequence.</p><p>Comparative analysis of <i>G. raimondii</i> with <i>T. cacao</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 20" title="Argout, X. et al. The genome of Theobroma cacao. Nat. Genet. 43, 101â108 (2011)." href="/articles/ng.2371#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d22850e1407">20</a></sup>, <i>A. thaliana</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 22" title="The Arabidopsis Genome Initiative. Analysis of the genome sequence of the flowering plant Arabidopsis thaliana. Nature 408, 796â815 (2000)." href="/articles/ng.2371#ref-CR22" id="ref-link-section-d22850e1413">22</a></sup> and <i>Z. mays</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 23" title="International Rice Sequencing Project. The map-based sequence of the rice genome. Nature 436, 793â800 (2005)." href="/articles/ng.2371#ref-CR23" id="ref-link-section-d22850e1419">23</a></sup> showed that these four different plant species possess similar numbers of gene families, with a core set of 9,525 in common (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2371#MOESM1">Supplementary Fig. 4</a>). Of the 16,113 <i>G. raimondii</i> gene families, all but 1,267 were conserved in at least 1 other plant genome (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2371#MOESM1">Supplementary Fig. 4</a>). Analysis of species- and lineage-specific families identified potential inconsistencies between annotation projects but also reflected genuine biological differences in gene inventories.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec5">Phylogeny, paleohexaploidization and whole-genome duplication</h3><p>Although large-scale duplication events were predicted to have occurred during <i>Gossypium</i> evolution, the number and timing of these genome duplications are still being debated<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 24" title="Senchina, D.S. et al. Rate variation among nuclear genes and the age of polyploidy in Gossypium. Mol. Biol. Evol. 20, 633â643 (2003)." href="/articles/ng.2371#ref-CR24" id="ref-link-section-d22850e1444">24</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 25" title="Blanc, G. &amp; Wolfe, K.H. Widespread paleopolyploidy in model plant species inferred from age distributions of duplicate genes. Plant Cell 16, 1667â1678 (2004)." href="/articles/ng.2371#ref-CR25" id="ref-link-section-d22850e1447">25</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 26" title="Fawcett, J.A., Maere, S. &amp; Van de Peer, Y. Plants with double genomes might have had a better chance to survive the Cretaceous-Tertiary extinction event. Proc. Natl. Acad. Sci. USA 106, 5737â5742 (2009)." href="/articles/ng.2371#ref-CR26" id="ref-link-section-d22850e1450">26</a></sup>. By examining 745 single-copy gene families from 9 sequenced plant genomes (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2371#MOESM1">Supplementary Fig. 5</a>), we found that <i>G. raimondii</i> and <i>T. cacao</i> belong to a common subclade and probably diverged from a common ancestor approximately 33.7 million years ago (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.2371#Fig2">Fig. 2a</a>). <i>Carica papaya</i> and <i>A. thaliana</i> belong to another subclade that diverged from the <i>G. raimondii</i>â<i>T. cacao</i> subclade approximately 82.3 million years ago (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.2371#Fig2">Fig. 2a</a>).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-2" data-title="Genome evolution and duplication."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig2" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 2: Genome evolution and duplication.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ng.2371/figures/2" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fng.2371/MediaObjects/41588_2012_Article_BFng2371_Fig2_HTML.jpg?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-2-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fng.2371/MediaObjects/41588_2012_Article_BFng2371_Fig2_HTML.jpg" alt="figure2" loading="lazy" width="685" height="799" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-2-desc"><p>(<b>a</b>) Phylogenetic analysis showed that <i>G. raimondii</i> and <i>T. cacao</i> separated approximately 33.7 million years ago (MYA). <i>O. sativa</i>, a monocot, was used as the outgroup. (<b>b</b>) Ks distributions of <i>G. raimondii</i>. Yellow line, Ks of all paralogous gene pairs; black line, Ks of tandem gene pairs only; green line, Ks of all except tandem gene pairs.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ng.2371/figures/2" data-track-dest="link:Figure2 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>Using substitution per synonymous site (Ks) values obtained from 3,195 paralogous gene pairs in the <i>G. raimondii</i> and <i>T. cacao</i> genomes, we observed 2 peaks at Ks values of 0.40â0.60 and 1.5â1.90 (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.2371#Fig2">Fig. 2b</a>). The first peak appeared at approximately 16.6 (13.3â20.0) million years ago, corresponding to the whole-genome duplication event that was previously proposed in the <i>Gossypium</i> lineage<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 25" title="Blanc, G. &amp; Wolfe, K.H. Widespread paleopolyploidy in model plant species inferred from age distributions of duplicate genes. Plant Cell 16, 1667â1678 (2004)." href="/articles/ng.2371#ref-CR25" id="ref-link-section-d22850e1537">25</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 26" title="Fawcett, J.A., Maere, S. &amp; Van de Peer, Y. Plants with double genomes might have had a better chance to survive the Cretaceous-Tertiary extinction event. Proc. Natl. Acad. Sci. USA 106, 5737â5742 (2009)." href="/articles/ng.2371#ref-CR26" id="ref-link-section-d22850e1540">26</a></sup>. The second peak appeared at approximately 130.8 (115.4â146.1) million years ago, corresponding to the paleohexaploidization event shared by the eudicots<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 27" title="Tang, H. et al. Unraveling ancient hexaploidy through multiple-aligned angiosperm gene maps. Genome Res. 18, 1944â1954 (2008)." href="/articles/ng.2371#ref-CR27" id="ref-link-section-d22850e1545">27</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 28" title="Van de Peer, Y., Fawcett, J.A., Proost, S., Sterck, L. &amp; Vandepoele, K. The flowering world: a tale of duplications. Trends Plant Sci. 14, 680â688 (2009)." href="/articles/ng.2371#ref-CR28" id="ref-link-section-d22850e1548">28</a></sup>. In <i>T. cacao</i>, a single peak value between 1.7â1.9 has been reported<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 20" title="Argout, X. et al. The genome of Theobroma cacao. Nat. Genet. 43, 101â108 (2011)." href="/articles/ng.2371#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d22850e1555">20</a></sup>, which corresponds to the second peak observed in <i>G. raimondii</i> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.2371#Fig2">Fig. 2b</a>), indicating that the paleohexaploidization event shared by the eudicots occurred between 115.4 and 146.1 million years ago in a common progenitor before speciation into the two present-day species 33.7 million years ago.</p><p>Comprehensive searches for evidence of whole-genome duplication were performed using an all-versus-all blastp approach comparing the <i>G. raimondii</i> and <i>T. cacao</i> genomes. Results indicated that the two genomes possess a moderate syntenic relationship, such that 463 collinear blocks (with â¥5 genes per block) covering 64.8% and 74.41% of the assembled <i>G. raimondii</i> and <i>T. cacao</i> genomes, respectively, are aligned (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.2371#Fig3">Fig. 3a</a>, <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2371#MOESM1">Supplementary Fig. 6</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2371#MOESM1">Supplementary Table 9a</a>). Reciprocal best-BLAST-match analysis showed the existence of 133 duplicated and 43 triplicated regions in <i>G. raimondii</i> relative to <i>T. cacao</i> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.2371#Fig3">Fig. 3a</a>). There were 2,355 syntenic blocks among the 13 <i>G. raimondii</i> chromosomes. Among these blocks, 21.2% were found to involve only two chromosome regions, 33.7% spanned three chromosome regions and 16.2% traversed four chromosome regions (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.2371#Fig3">Fig. 3b</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2371#MOESM1">Supplementary Fig. 7</a>). Chromosome 8 was highly fragmented, with 310 blocks that matched other chromosomes, probably as a result of multiple rounds of duplication, diploidization and chromosomal rearrangement in the genome (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.2371#Fig3">Fig. 3b</a>). Thirty-nine triplicated chromosomal regions in the <i>G. raimondii</i> genome were observed (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2371#MOESM2">Supplementary Table 9b</a>).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-3" data-title="Comparison of syntenic blocks between the genomes of T. cacao and G. raimondii and reorganization of G. raimondii chromosomes."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig3" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 3: Comparison of syntenic blocks between the genomes of <i>T. cacao</i> and <i>G. raimondii</i> and reorganization of <i>G. raimondii</i> chromosomes.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ng.2371/figures/3" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fng.2371/MediaObjects/41588_2012_Article_BFng2371_Fig3_HTML.jpg?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-3-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fng.2371/MediaObjects/41588_2012_Article_BFng2371_Fig3_HTML.jpg" alt="figure3" loading="lazy" width="685" height="372" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-3-desc"><p>(<b>a</b>) Syntenic blocks between <i>T. cacao</i> and <i>G. raimondii</i>. <i>Tc</i>, chromosome of <i>T. cacao</i>; <i>Gr</i>, chromosome of <i>G. raimondii</i>. (<b>b</b>) Syntenic blocks among different <i>G. raimondii</i> chromosomes. The <i>G. raimondii</i> chromosomes are shown in the outer circle in mosaic form, with each color designating its origin from one of the seven ancient chromosomes. Only syntenic blocks longer than 700 kb are shown.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ng.2371/figures/3" data-track-dest="link:Figure3 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec6">Expansion of transposable elements</h3><p>Transposable elements are known to contribute substantially to changes in genome size, and they comprise approximately 57% (441 Mb in total length) of the <i>G. raimondii</i> genome (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/ng.2371#Tab1">Table 1</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2371#MOESM1">Supplementary Table 10</a>). In comparison, 24% of the <i>T. cacao</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 20" title="Argout, X. et al. The genome of Theobroma cacao. Nat. Genet. 43, 101â108 (2011)." href="/articles/ng.2371#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d22850e1698">20</a></sup> genome and 14% of the <i>A. thaliana</i> genome are composed of transposable elements<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 22" title="The Arabidopsis Genome Initiative. Analysis of the genome sequence of the flowering plant Arabidopsis thaliana. Nature 408, 796â815 (2000)." href="/articles/ng.2371#ref-CR22" id="ref-link-section-d22850e1706">22</a></sup>, suggesting that substantial transposable element proliferation in <i>G. raimondii</i> is partially accountable for the expansion of the <i>G. raimondii</i> genome. In-depth sequence analysis showed that the most widespread repetitive sequences in the <i>G. raimondii</i> genome were the <i>gypsy</i> and <i>copia</i>-like LTRs, which account for 33.83% and 11.10% of the genome, respectively (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2371#MOESM1">Supplementary Table 10</a>). The growth rate of these LTR retrotransposons in <i>G. raimondii</i> and <i>T. cacao</i> tended to slow down after 0.5 and 0.7 million years ago, respectively (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.2371#Fig4">Fig. 4a</a>). By contrast, the number of LTR retrotransposons has increased in <i>A. thaliana</i> since 1.5 million years ago (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.2371#Fig4">Fig. 4a</a>).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-4" data-title="Comparisons of LTRs and transposable elements in the G. raimondii, T. cacao and A. thaliana genomes."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig4" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 4: Comparisons of LTRs and transposable elements in the <i>G. raimondii</i>, <i>T. cacao</i> and <i>A. thaliana</i> genomes.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ng.2371/figures/4" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fng.2371/MediaObjects/41588_2012_Article_BFng2371_Fig4_HTML.jpg?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-4-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fng.2371/MediaObjects/41588_2012_Article_BFng2371_Fig4_HTML.jpg" alt="figure4" loading="lazy" width="685" height="981" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-4-desc"><p>(<b>a</b>) The distribution curve for the number and insertion time of LTRs in different plant genomes. (<b>b</b>) Phylogeny of LTR retrotransposons in the <i>G. raimondii</i>, <i>T. cacao</i> and <i>A. thaliana</i> genomes. (<b>c</b>) Distance distributions of nearest transposable elements (TEs) from each gene.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ng.2371/figures/4" data-track-dest="link:Figure4 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>Phylogenetic analysis supported the notion that a larger expansion of specific LTR retrotransposon clades had occurred in <i>G. raimondii</i> than in <i>T. cacao</i> and <i>A. thaliana</i> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.2371#Fig4">Fig. 4b</a>). An analysis of the repeat divergence rate distribution (percentage of substitutions in the corresponding region compared with consensus repeats in constructed libraries) independently confirmed the proliferation pattern for LTR retrotransposons in the <i>G. raimondii</i> genome (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2371#MOESM1">Supplementary Fig. 8</a>). Coupled with higher transposable element content in its genome, <i>G. raimondii</i> was found to have a higher proportion of genes near (within 1 kb of) transposable elements than <i>T. cacao</i> and <i>A. thaliana</i> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.2371#Fig4">Fig. 4c</a>). By contrast, <i>T. cacao</i> maintained the greatest distance between its genes and transposable elements (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.2371#Fig4">Fig. 4c</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec7">Simple sequence repeats (SSRs) in the <i>G. raimondii</i> genome</h3><p>SSRs behave as polymorphic loci that provide a rich source of markers for cotton breeding as well as for genetic studies. A total of 15,503 di-, tri- and tetranucleotide SSRs, representing 34 distinctive motif families, were identified and annotated in the <i>G. raimondii</i> genome (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2371#MOESM1">Supplementary Fig. 9</a>). We randomly selected 500 of them to study polymorphisms between the mapping parents <i>G. hirsutum</i> 'CCRI36' and <i>G. barbadense</i> 'Hai1' and found that 70 primer pairs, or 14%, showed polymorphisms. PCR amplification results for 15 of these primer pairs are shown in <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2371#MOESM1">Supplementary Figure 10</a>.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec8">Analysis of genes involved in cotton fiber initiation and elongation</h3><p>Qualitative transcript differences in key fiber development genes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2" title="Ruan, Y.L., Llewellyn, D.J. &amp; Furbank, R.T. Suppression of sucrose synthase gene expression represses cotton fiber cell initiation, elongation, and seed development. Plant Cell 15, 952â964 (2003)." href="/articles/ng.2371#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d22850e1871">2</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 3" title="Shi, Y.H. et al. Transcriptome profiling, molecular biological, and physiological studies reveal a major role for ethylene in cotton fiber cell elongation. Plant Cell 18, 651â664 (2006)." href="/articles/ng.2371#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d22850e1874">3</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 29" title="Qin, Y.M. et al. Saturated very-long-chain fatty acids promote cotton fiber and Arabidopsis cell elongation by activating ethylene biosynthesis. Plant Cell 19, 3692â3704 (2007)." href="/articles/ng.2371#ref-CR29" id="ref-link-section-d22850e1877">29</a></sup> were found between the non-fibered <i>G. raimondii</i> and the fibered <i>G. hirsutum</i> species, as revealed by transcriptome (RNA sequencing, RNA-seq) analysis using samples extracted from cotton ovules 3 days post-anthesis (DPA). Of the four sucrose synthase (Sus) genes identified in the genome, three (<i>SusB</i>, <i>Sus1</i> and <i>SusD</i>) were expressed at substantially higher levels in <i>G. hirsutum</i> than in <i>G. raimondii</i> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.2371#Fig5">Fig. 5a</a>). Several 3-ketoacyl-CoA synthase (KCS) genes, including <i>KCS2</i>, <i>KCS13</i> and <i>KCS6</i>, were only expressed in <i>G. hirsutum</i>, whereas intermediate levels of <i>KCS7</i> transcripts were observed in both <i>G. hirsutum</i> and <i>G. raimondii</i> (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.2371#Fig5">Fig. 5b</a>), indicating that high-level expression of Sus and KCS family genes may indeed be required for fiber cell initiation and elongation. By contrast, extremely high amounts of transcripts encoding 1-aminocyclopropane-1-carboxylic acid oxidase (ACO) activities were recovered from <i>G. raimondii</i> at the 3-DPA stage (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.2371#Fig5">Fig. 5c</a>), which is suggestive of a major role for the plant hormone ethylene during early fiber cell development.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-5" data-title="Topological trees and expression patterns of Sus, KCS, ACO, MYB and bHLH family genes in the transcriptome of G. raimondii and G. hirsutum."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig5" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 5: Topological trees and expression patterns of Sus, KCS, ACO, MYB and bHLH family genes in the transcriptome of <i>G. raimondii</i> and <i>G. hirsutum</i>.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ng.2371/figures/5" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fng.2371/MediaObjects/41588_2012_Article_BFng2371_Fig5_HTML.jpg?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-5-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fng.2371/MediaObjects/41588_2012_Article_BFng2371_Fig5_HTML.jpg" alt="figure5" loading="lazy" width="685" height="668" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-5-desc"><p>(<b>a</b>) Major sucrose synthase genes (Sus) were expressed at substantially higher levels in <i>G. hirsutum</i> ovules with developing fiber initials than in those of <i>G. raimondii</i>. (<b>b</b>) Substantially more 3-ketoacyl-CoA synthase (KCS) transcripts were found in <i>G. hirsutum</i> ovules. (<b>c</b>) Substantially more 1-aminocyclopropane-1-carboxylic acid oxidase (ACO) transcripts were found in <i>G. raimondii</i> ovules. (<b>d</b>) <i>G. hirsutum</i> preferentially expressed MYB transcription factors. (<b>e</b>) <i>G. hirsutum</i> preferentially expressed bHLH transcription factors. Shown in each panel are the topological tree (left) and comparison of expression levels (right) between the two cotton species. Expression levels were estimated by reads per kilobase of mapped cDNA per million reads (RPKM) values for each gene obtained by sequencing RNA samples from 3-DPA <i>G. raimondii</i> and <i>G. hirsutum</i> ovules.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ng.2371/figures/5" data-track-dest="link:Figure5 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>Previous researchers have postulated that the cotton fiber is similar in form and origin to plant trichomes, hair-like epidermal cells that occur on various plant organs but are common to leaf and stem surfaces. As postulated, transcription factors that have important roles in <i>A. thaliana</i> trichome development may be related to factors involved in cotton fiber formation<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Wang, S. et al. Control of plant trichome development by a cotton fiber MYB gene. Plant Cell 16, 2323â2334 (2004)." href="/articles/ng.2371#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d22850e2012">4</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 30" title="Larkin, J.C., Oppenheimer, D.G., Lloyd, A.M., Paparozzi, E.T. &amp; Marks, M.D. Roles of the GLABROUS1 and TRANSPARENT TESTA GLABRA genes in Arabidopsis trichome development. Plant Cell 6, 1065â1076 (1994)." href="/articles/ng.2371#ref-CR30" id="ref-link-section-d22850e2015">30</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 31" title="Walford, S.-A., Wu, Y., Llewellyn, D.J. &amp; Dennis, E.S. GhMYB25-like: a key factor in early cotton fibre development. Plant J. 65, 785â797 (2011)." href="/articles/ng.2371#ref-CR31" id="ref-link-section-d22850e2018">31</a></sup>. In <i>A. thaliana</i>, MYB and bHLH class transcription factors work in a complex in combination with TTG1 to specify a particular epidermal cell fate<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 30" title="Larkin, J.C., Oppenheimer, D.G., Lloyd, A.M., Paparozzi, E.T. &amp; Marks, M.D. Roles of the GLABROUS1 and TRANSPARENT TESTA GLABRA genes in Arabidopsis trichome development. Plant Cell 6, 1065â1076 (1994)." href="/articles/ng.2371#ref-CR30" id="ref-link-section-d22850e2025">30</a></sup>. A total of 2,706 transcription factors, including 208 bHLH and 219 MYB class genes, were identified in the <i>G. raimondii</i> genome (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2371#MOESM1">Supplementary Table 11</a>). A large number of MYB (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.2371#Fig5">Fig. 5d</a>) and bHLH (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.2371#Fig5">Fig. 5e</a>) genes were expressed predominantly in <i>G. hirsutum</i> ovules, with only remnant levels found in the ovules of <i>G. raimondii</i>, indicating that some of these genes may be required for early fiber development.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec9">Gossypol biosynthesis genes</h3><p>Cotton is known to produce a unique group of terpenoids that include desoxyhemigossypol, hemigossypol, gossypol, hemigossypolone and the heliocides. Cotton plants accumulate gossypol and related sesquiterpenoids in pigment glands as a defense against pathogens and herbivores. The majority of cotton sesquiterpenoids are derived from a common precursor, (+)-Î´-cadinene, which is synthesized by (+)-Î´-cadinene synthase (CDN) via cyclization of farnesyl diphosphate, in the first committed step in gossypol biosynthesis<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 32" title="Essenberg, M., Grover, P.B. Jr. &amp; Cover, E.C. Accumulation of antibacterial sesquiterpenoids in bacterially inoculated Gossypium leaves and cotyledons. Phytochemistry 29, 3107â3113 (1990)." href="/articles/ng.2371#ref-CR32" id="ref-link-section-d22850e2056">32</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 33" title="Chen, X.Y., Chen, Y., Heinstein, P. &amp; Davisson, V.J. Cloning, expression, and characterization of (+)-Î´-cadinene synthase: a catalyst for cotton phytoalexin biosynthesis. Arch. Biochem. Biophys. 324, 255â266 (1995)." href="/articles/ng.2371#ref-CR33" id="ref-link-section-d22850e2059">33</a></sup>. Previously, both CDN-A and CDN-C were reported to encode the proposed enzyme activity<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 34" title="Chen, X.Y., Wang, M., Chen, Y., Davisson, V.J. &amp; Heinstein, P. Cloning and heterologous expression of a second (+)-Î´-cadinene synthase from Gossypium arboreum. J. Nat. Prod. 59, 944â951 (1996)." href="/articles/ng.2371#ref-CR34" id="ref-link-section-d22850e2063">34</a></sup>. Phylogenetic analysis performed here using <i>G. raimondii</i> and eight other sequenced plant genomes, including <i>T. cacao</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 20" title="Argout, X. et al. The genome of Theobroma cacao. Nat. Genet. 43, 101â108 (2011)." href="/articles/ng.2371#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d22850e2072">20</a></sup>, <i>A. thaliana</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 22" title="The Arabidopsis Genome Initiative. Analysis of the genome sequence of the flowering plant Arabidopsis thaliana. Nature 408, 796â815 (2000)." href="/articles/ng.2371#ref-CR22" id="ref-link-section-d22850e2079">22</a></sup>, <i>Oryza sativa</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 23" title="International Rice Sequencing Project. The map-based sequence of the rice genome. Nature 436, 793â800 (2005)." href="/articles/ng.2371#ref-CR23" id="ref-link-section-d22850e2085">23</a></sup>, <i>C. papaya</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 35" title="Ming, R. et al. The draft genome of the transgenic tropical fruit tree papaya (Carica papaya Linnaeus). Nature 452, 991â996 (2008)." href="/articles/ng.2371#ref-CR35" id="ref-link-section-d22850e2091">35</a></sup>, <i>Vitis vinifera</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 36" title="French-Italian Public Consortium for Grapevine Genome Characterization. The grapevine genome sequence suggests ancestral hexaploidization in major angiosperm phyla. Nature 449, 463â467 (2007)." href="/articles/ng.2371#ref-CR36" id="ref-link-section-d22850e2097">36</a></sup>, <i>Populus trichocarpa</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 37" title="Tuskan, G.A. et al. The genome of black cottonwood, Populus trichocarpa (Torr. &amp; Gray). Science 313, 1596â1604 (2006)." href="/articles/ng.2371#ref-CR37" id="ref-link-section-d22850e2104">37</a></sup>, <i>Glycine max</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 38" title="Schmutz, J. et al. Genome sequence of the palaeopolyploid soybean. Nature 463, 178â183 (2010)." href="/articles/ng.2371#ref-CR38" id="ref-link-section-d22850e2110">38</a></sup> and <i>Ricinus communis</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 39" title="Chan, A.P. et al. Draft genome sequence of the oilseed species Ricinus communis. Nat. Biotechnol. 28, 951â956 (2010)." href="/articles/ng.2371#ref-CR39" id="ref-link-section-d22850e2116">39</a></sup>, showed that, except for <i>O. sativa</i>, terpene cyclase gene families are common in various plant species (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.2371#Fig6">Fig. 6</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2371#MOESM1">Supplementary Fig. 11</a>). However, <i>G. raimondii</i> and probably <i>T. cacao</i> were the only plant species that possess an authentic <i>CDN1</i> gene family with the proposed biochemical function (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.2371#Fig6">Fig. 6</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/ng.2371#MOESM1">Supplementary Fig. 11</a>). It seemed that the ability to synthesize gossypol is related to both the paleohexaploidization and the whole-genome duplication events that were observed (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/ng.2371#Fig2">Fig. 2b</a>). No <i>CDN1</i> orthologs were found in <i>P. trichocarpa</i> or <i>C. papaya</i>, the most closely related subclade, suggesting that gossypol production evolved after the separation of these plant species. This conclusion was supported by a recent publication that indicated the key importance of two aspartate-rich Mg<sup>2+</sup>-binding motifs, DDtYD and DDVAE, for gossypol biosynthesis<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 40" title="Gennadios, H.A. et al. Crystal structure of (+)-Î´-cadinene synthase from Gossypium arboretum and evolutionary divergence of metal binding motifs for catalysis. Biochemistry 48, 6175â6183 (2009)." href="/articles/ng.2371#ref-CR40" id="ref-link-section-d22850e2161">40</a></sup>. All other plant terpene cyclase genes do not encode proteins with the DDVAE motif and thus cannot be recognized as CDN orthologs.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-6" data-title="Phylogenetic analysis of the CDN1 gene family in G. max, P. trichocarpa, A. thaliana, C. papaya, V. vinifera, R. communis, T. cacao and G. raimondii."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig6" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Figure 6: Phylogenetic analysis of the <i>CDN1</i> gene family in <i>G. max</i>, <i>P. trichocarpa</i>, <i>A. thaliana</i>, <i>C. papaya</i>, <i>V. vinifera</i>, <i>R. communis</i>, <i>T. cacao</i> and <i>G. raimondii</i>.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ng.2371/figures/6" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fng.2371/MediaObjects/41588_2012_Article_BFng2371_Fig6_HTML.jpg?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-6-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fng.2371/MediaObjects/41588_2012_Article_BFng2371_Fig6_HTML.jpg" alt="figure6" loading="lazy" width="685" height="348" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-6-desc"><p>The phylogenetic tree and multiple-sequence alignment were established using the neighbor-joining method with Mega 4 software<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 42" title="Tamura, K., Dudley, J., Nei, M. &amp; Kumar, S. MEGA4: molecular evolutionary genetics analysis (MEGA) software version 4.0. Mol. Biol. Evol. 24, 1596â1599 (2007)." href="/articles/ng.2371#ref-CR42" id="ref-link-section-d22850e2206">42</a></sup>. Bootstrap numbers greater than 50 are shown on the branches.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/ng.2371/figures/6" data-track-dest="link:Figure6 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec10" data-title="Discussion"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec10-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec10">Discussion</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec10-content"><p>We have sequenced the genome of <i>G. raimondii</i> using a next-generation Illumina paired-end sequencing strategy, yielding an assembled sequence with 103.6-fold genome coverage. The draft sequence covered 88.1% of the estimated <i>G. raimondii</i> genome size. Compared with other sequenced plant genomes, <i>G. raimondii</i> showed substantially lower gene density with a high proportion of transposable elements despite being one of the smallest <i>Gossypium</i> genomes. One independent whole-genome duplication event occurred approximately 13.3 to 20.0 million years ago, and one paleohexaploidization event that is commonly found in eudicots was clearly observed in the <i>G. raimondii</i> genome. The dates of these events reported here agree with those proposed in previous studies<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 25" title="Blanc, G. &amp; Wolfe, K.H. Widespread paleopolyploidy in model plant species inferred from age distributions of duplicate genes. Plant Cell 16, 1667â1678 (2004)." href="/articles/ng.2371#ref-CR25" id="ref-link-section-d22850e2243">25</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 26" title="Fawcett, J.A., Maere, S. &amp; Van de Peer, Y. Plants with double genomes might have had a better chance to survive the Cretaceous-Tertiary extinction event. Proc. Natl. Acad. Sci. USA 106, 5737â5742 (2009)." href="/articles/ng.2371#ref-CR26" id="ref-link-section-d22850e2246">26</a></sup>. <i>G. hirsutum</i>, an allotetraploid species, is believed to be the product of a hybridization of two parental diploid species with A and D genomes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 41" title="Wendel, J.F. &amp; Cronn, R.C. Polyploidy and the evolutionary history of cotton. Adv. Agron. 78, 139â186 (2003)." href="/articles/ng.2371#ref-CR41" id="ref-link-section-d22850e2253">41</a></sup>. An average Ks value of 0.042 was previously reported for tetraploid formation on the basis of an analysis of 42 pairs of paralogous <i>G. hirsutum</i> genes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 24" title="Senchina, D.S. et al. Rate variation among nuclear genes and the age of polyploidy in Gossypium. Mol. Biol. Evol. 20, 633â643 (2003)." href="/articles/ng.2371#ref-CR24" id="ref-link-section-d22850e2260">24</a></sup>.</p><p>Qualitative differences were found for genes encoding Sus, KCS and ACO activities by comparing the transcriptomes of fiber-bearing <i>G. hirsutum</i> and the non-fibered <i>G. raimondii</i>. These results indicate that Sus, KCS and ACO are necessary for cotton fiber development, as was proposed in previous individual studies<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2" title="Ruan, Y.L., Llewellyn, D.J. &amp; Furbank, R.T. Suppression of sucrose synthase gene expression represses cotton fiber cell initiation, elongation, and seed development. Plant Cell 15, 952â964 (2003)." href="/articles/ng.2371#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d22850e2273">2</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 3" title="Shi, Y.H. et al. Transcriptome profiling, molecular biological, and physiological studies reveal a major role for ethylene in cotton fiber cell elongation. Plant Cell 18, 651â664 (2006)." href="/articles/ng.2371#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d22850e2276">3</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 29" title="Qin, Y.M. et al. Saturated very-long-chain fatty acids promote cotton fiber and Arabidopsis cell elongation by activating ethylene biosynthesis. Plant Cell 19, 3692â3704 (2007)." href="/articles/ng.2371#ref-CR29" id="ref-link-section-d22850e2279">29</a></sup>. Also, the MYB and bHLH transcription factors preferentially expressed in fiber reported herein may be used to elucidate the molecular mechanisms governing fiber initiation and early cell growth. Greater understanding of gossypol and related sesquiterpenoid biosynthesis genes may enable engineering of these genes for better defense against pathogens and herbivores in the cotton field. We suggest that sequencing of the <i>G. raimondii</i> genome is a major step toward fully deciphering and analyzing the genomes of the <i>Gossypium</i> family to improve cotton productivity and fiber quality.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec11">URLs.</h3><p>Genome browser for <i>G. raimondii</i> at the Cotton Genome Project, <a href="http://cgp.genomics.org.cn/">http://cgp.genomics.org.cn/</a><i>G. raimondii</i> genome sequencing data at NCBI BioProject, <a href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/bioproject/?term=%20PRJNA82769">http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/bioproject/?term=%20PRJNA82769</a>; MarketPublishers, <a href="http://marketpublishers.com/">http://marketpublishers.com/</a>; CocoaGen DB, <a href="http://cocoagendb.cirad.fr/">http://cocoagendb.cirad.fr/</a>; <i>Arabidopsis</i> Information Resource, <a href="http://www.arabidopsis.org/">http://www.arabidopsis.org/</a>; The Rice Annotation Project Database, <a href="http://rapdb.dna.affrc.go.jp/">http://rapdb.dna.affrc.go.jp/</a>; The Hawaii Papaya Genome Project, <a href="http://asgpb.mhpcc.hawaii.edu/papaya/">http://asgpb.mhpcc.hawaii.edu/papaya/</a>; genome assembly of <i>V. vinifera</i>, <a href="http://www.genoscope.cns.fr/spip/Vitis-vinifera-e.html">http://www.genoscope.cns.fr/spip/Vitis-vinifera-e.html</a>; genome assembly of <i>G. max</i>, <a href="http://www.phytozome.net/soybean">http://www.phytozome.net/soybean</a>; Castor Bean Genome Database, <a href="http://castorbean.jcvi.org/">http://castorbean.jcvi.org/</a>; genome assembly of <i>P. trichocarpa</i>, <a href="http://www.phytozome.net/poplar">http://www.phytozome.net/poplar</a>; SOAPdenovo, <a href="http://soap.genomics.org.cn/">http://soap.genomics.org.cn/</a>; estclean, <a href="https://sourceforge.net/projects/estclean/">https://sourceforge.net/projects/estclean/</a>; SSPACE, <a href="http://www.baseclear.com/landingpages/sspacev12/">http://www.baseclear.com/landingpages/sspacev12/</a>.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec12" data-title="Methods"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec12-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec12">Methods</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec12-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec13">Germplasm genetic resources.</h3><p>DNA samples of the D genome were obtained from CMD 10 (refs. <a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 15" title="Yu, J.Z. A standard panel of Gossypium genotypes established for systematic characterization of cotton microsatellite markers. Plant Breeding News 148, 1.07 (2004)." href="/articles/ng.2371#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d22850e2426">15</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 16" title="Blenda, A. et al. CMD: a cotton microsatellite database resource for Gossypium genomics. BMC Genomics 7, 132â141 (2006)." href="/articles/ng.2371#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d22850e2429">16</a>), a genetic standard that originated from a single seed (accession D<sub>5</sub>-3) in 2004 and was brought to near homozygosity by six successive generations of self-fertilization in the greenhouse. <i>G. raimondii</i> D<sub>5</sub>-3 (CMD 10) was maintained in the nursery on the China National Wild Cotton Plantation in Sanya, and the <i>G. hirsutum</i> genetic standard, TM-1 (CMD 1), was grown under standard greenhouse conditions with the temperature maintained at 32 Â°C during the day time. Fresh young leaves were collected, immediately frozen in liquid nitrogen and stored at â80 Â°C until DNA extraction.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec14">DNA extraction, library construction and sequencing.</h3><p>We used the standard phenol/chloroform method for DNA extraction, with RNase A and proteinase K treatment to prevent RNA and protein contamination. The extracted DNA was then precipitated with ethanol. Genomic libraries were prepared following the manufacturer's standard instructions and sequenced on the Illumina HiSeq 2000 platform. To construct the paired-end libraries, DNA was fragmented by nebulization with compressed nitrogen gas, the DNA ends were blunted and an A base was added to the 3â² ends. DNA adaptors with a single T-base 3â²-end overhang were ligated to the above products. Ligation products were purified on 0.5%, 1% or 2% agarose gels targeted for each specific insert size and were purified from the gels (Qiagen Gel Extraction kit, 28704). We constructed <i>G. raimondii</i> genome sequencing libraries with insert sizes of 170 bp, 250 bp, 500 bp, 800 bp, 2 kb, 5 kb, 10 kb, 20 kb and 40 kb.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec15">Genome assembly.</h3><p>The <i>G. raimondii</i> genome was assembled using SOAPdenovo with a <i>K</i>-mer of 41 and SSPACE software. We first assembled the reads with short insert size (&lt;2 kb) to obtain long contigs. Then, the reads with long insert sizes (&lt;40 kb) were aligned to the contigs to form scaffolds. Finally, we used the paired-end relationships of 40,000 library reads to construct super-scaffolds.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec16">Chromosome anchoring.</h3><p>We aligned the marker sequences from the cotton consensus map<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="Lin, L. et al. A draft physical map of a D-genome cotton species (Gossypium raimondii). BMC Genomics 11, 395 (2010)." href="/articles/ng.2371#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d22850e2477">17</a></sup> to the scaffolds using blastn (identities â¥ 95%; <i>e</i> value â¤ 1.0 Ã 10<sup>â6</sup>; coverage â¥ 85%), and the best-scoring match was chosen in cases of multiple matches.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec17">Genome synteny and whole-genome duplication analysis.</h3><p>We use blastp (identity â¥ 40%; <i>e</i> value â¤ 1.0 Ã 10<sup>â5</sup>; match length of more than 100 amino acids) to detect paralogous genes in <i>G. raimondii</i> and <i>T. cacao</i>, and we applied OrthoMCL to detect gene families<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 43" title="Li, L., Stoeckert, C.J. Jr. &amp; Roos, D.S. OrthoMCL: identification of ortholog groups for eukaryotic genomes. Genome Res. 13, 2178â2189 (2003)." href="/articles/ng.2371#ref-CR43" id="ref-link-section-d22850e2506">43</a></sup>. For each paralogous gene family, the Ks of each pair was calculated using the PAML package<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 44" title="Yang, Z. PAML 4: phylogenetic analysis by maximum likelihood. Mol. Biol. Evol. 24, 1586â1591 (2007)." href="/articles/ng.2371#ref-CR44" id="ref-link-section-d22850e2511">44</a></sup>, and the median was selected to represent the Ks of the family.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec18">RNA-seq analysis.</h3><p>Total RNA was isolated from 0-DPA ovules, 3-DPA ovules of <i>G. raimondii</i> D<sub>5</sub>-3 (CMD 10) and <i>G. hirsutum</i> TM-1 (CMD 1) and from mature leaves of <i>G. raimondii</i> D<sub>5</sub>-3 (CMD 10). Normalized pools were converted to full-length enriched cDNA using the SMART method and were sequenced using Illumina protocols. All reads were filtered to trim the adaptor sequences using estclean. Clean reads (with at least 20 nucleotides remaining after trimming) were then mapped to the <i>G. raimondii</i> gene models using CLC Genomics Workbench software 4 (CLC bio A/S Science), and matches were converted to RPKM to estimate gene expression levels.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec19">Accession codes.</h3><p><i>G. raimondii</i> genome sequencing data are available at NCBI BioProject under accession <a href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/bioproject/?term=PRJNA82769">PRJNA82769</a>. Sequencing data for <i>G. raimondii</i> and <i>G. hirsutum</i> transcriptome analyses are available in the NCBI Sequence Read Archive (SRA) under accessions <a href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/sra?term=SRA048621">SRA048621</a> and <a href="http://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/sra?term=SRA048874">SRA048874</a>.</p></div></div></section>

L.C., S.Z., B.W., Junyi Wang, Y. Yin, C.S. and N.S. performed sequencing, assembly and genome annotation. K.W., Z.W., F.L., Jun Wang, W.Y., C.G., Y. Yuan and Z.Y. managed the project. Y. Yuan, H.S., C.Z. and Q.L. performed the genome assembly and physical map integration. C.L., H.W., C.Z., H.S., K.L., X.Z. and Z.Z. prepared DNA and RNA samples. R.J.K., R.G.P. and J.Z.Y. conceived the project, provided the homozygous seeds and revised the manuscript. Y.-X.Z., H.S., C.Z. and Q.L. performed transcriptome and linage-specific gene functional analyses. Y.-X.Z., H.S., C.Z., Q.L. and W.Y. wrote and revised the manuscript.
